<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Seventh planet from the Sun</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the planet. For the Greek god, see <a href="/wiki/Uranus_(mythology)" title="Uranus (mythology)">Uranus (mythology)</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Uranus_(disambiguation)" title="Uranus (disambiguation)">Uranus (disambiguation)</a>. and <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Uranian_(disambiguation)" title="Uranian (disambiguation)">Uranian (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Seventh planet" redirects here. For other systems of numbering planets, see <a href="/wiki/Planet#History" title="Planet">Planet § History</a>.</div>

Icy <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a>: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r979066050">.mw-parser-output ul.cslist,.mw-parser-output ul.sslist{margin:0;padding:0;display:inline-block;list-style:none}.mw-parser-output ul.cslist-embedded{display:inline}.mw-parser-output .cslist li,.mw-parser-output .sslist li{margin:0;padding:0 0.25em 0 0;display:inline-block}.mw-parser-output .cslist li:after{content:", "}.mw-parser-output .sslist li:after{content:"; "}.mw-parser-output .cslist li:last-child:after,.mw-parser-output .sslist li:last-child:after{content:none}</style><ul class="cslist"><li><a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">water ice</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a></li><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methane_hydrate" title="Methane hydrate">methane hydrate</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Uranus</b> is the seventh <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. Its name is a reference to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_god" title="Greek god">Greek god</a> of the sky, <a href="/wiki/Uranus_(mythology)" title="Uranus (mythology)">Uranus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Caelus" title="Caelus">Caelus</a>), who, according to <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, was the great-grandfather of <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">Mars</a>), grandfather of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>) and father of <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">Saturn</a>). It has the third-largest planetary radius and fourth-largest planetary mass in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. Uranus is similar in composition to <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, and both have bulk chemical compositions which differ from that of the larger <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>. For this reason, scientists often classify Uranus and Neptune as "<a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a>" to distinguish them from the other giant planets. 
</p><p>As with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_giants" title="Gas giants">gas giants</a>, ice giants also lack a well defined "solid surface." Uranus's <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere#Others" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> is similar to Jupiter's and Saturn's in its primary composition of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, but it contains more "<a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">ices</a>" such as water, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, along with traces of other <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup> It has the coldest planetary atmosphere in the Solar System, with a minimum temperature of 49 K (−224 °C; −371 °F), and has a complex, layered <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">cloud</a> structure with water thought to make up the lowest clouds and methane the uppermost layer of clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup> The interior of Uranus is mainly composed of ices and rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like the other <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a>, Uranus has a <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Uranus" title="Rings of Uranus">ring system</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>, and numerous <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>. The Uranian system has a unique configuration because its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_of_rotation" title="Axis of rotation">axis of rotation</a> is tilted sideways, nearly into the plane of its solar orbit. Its north and south poles, therefore, lie where most other planets have their <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup> In 1986, images from <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> showed Uranus as an almost featureless planet in visible light, without the cloud bands or storms associated with the other giant planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup> <i>Voyager 2</i> remains the only spacecraft to visit the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[22]</a></sup> Observations from Earth have shown seasonal change and increased weather activity as Uranus approached its <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">equinox</a> in 2007. Wind speeds can reach 250 metres per second (900 km/h; 560 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>Like the <a href="/wiki/Classical_planet" title="Classical planet">classical planets</a>, Uranus is visible to the naked eye, but it was never recognised as a planet by ancient observers because of its dimness and slow orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[24]</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> first observed Uranus on 13 March 1781, leading to its discovery as a planet, expanding the known boundaries of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> for the first time in history and making Uranus the first planet classified as such with the aid of a <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="34_Tauri"></span>
</p><p>Uranus had been observed on many occasions before its recognition as a planet, but it was generally mistaken for a star. Possibly the earliest known observation was by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hipparchos" title="Hipparchos">Hipparchos</a>, who in 128 BC might have recorded it as a star for his <a href="/wiki/Star_catalogue" title="Star catalogue">star catalogue</a> that was later incorporated into <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[25]</a></sup> The earliest definite sighting was in 1690, when <a href="/wiki/John_Flamsteed" title="John Flamsteed">John Flamsteed</a> observed it at least six times, cataloguing it as 34 <a href="/wiki/Taurus_(constellation)" title="Taurus (constellation)">Tauri</a>. The French astronomer <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Charles_Le_Monnier" title="Pierre Charles Le Monnier">Pierre Charles Le Monnier</a> observed Uranus at least twelve times between 1750 and 1769,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[26]</a></sup> including on four consecutive nights.
</p><p>Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> observed Uranus on 13 March 1781 from the garden of his house at 19 New King Street in <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath, Somerset</a>, England (now the <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Museum_of_Astronomy" title="Herschel Museum of Astronomy">Herschel Museum of Astronomy</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[27]</a></sup> and initially reported it (on 26 April 1781) as a <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[28]</a></sup> With a homemade 6.2-inch reflecting telescope, Herschel "engaged in a series of observations on the <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">parallax</a> of the fixed stars."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref-1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ref-1-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Herschel recorded in his journal: "In the quartile near <a href="/wiki/Zeta_Tauri" title="Zeta Tauri">ζ Tauri</a> ... either [a] Nebulous star or perhaps a comet."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[31]</a></sup> On 17 March he noted: "I looked for the Comet or Nebulous Star and found that it is a Comet, for it has changed its place."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[32]</a></sup> When he presented his discovery to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, he continued to assert that he had found a comet, but also implicitly compared it to a planet:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref-1_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ref-1-34">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The power I had on when I first saw the comet was 227. From experience I know that the diameters of the fixed stars are not proportionally magnified with higher powers, as planets are; therefore I now put the powers at 460 and 932, and found that the diameter of the comet increased in proportion to the power, as it ought to be, on the supposition of its not being a fixed star, while the diameters of the stars to which I compared it were not increased in the same ratio. Moreover, the comet being magnified much beyond what its light would admit of, appeared hazy and ill-defined with these great powers, while the stars preserved that lustre and distinctness which from many thousand observations I knew they would retain. The sequel has shown that my surmises were well-founded, this proving to be the Comet we have lately observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref-1_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ref-1-34">[29]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Herschel notified the Astronomer Royal <a href="/wiki/Nevil_Maskelyne" title="Nevil Maskelyne">Nevil Maskelyne</a> of his discovery and received this flummoxed reply from him on 23 April 1781: "I don't know what to call it. It is as likely to be a regular planet moving in an orbit nearly circular to the sun as a Comet moving in a very eccentric ellipsis. I have not yet seen any coma or tail to it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Herschel continued to describe his new object as a comet, other astronomers had already begun to suspect otherwise. Finnish-Swedish astronomer <a href="/wiki/Anders_Johan_Lexell" title="Anders Johan Lexell">Anders Johan Lexell</a>, working in Russia, was the first to compute the orbit of the new object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lexell_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-lexell-39">[34]</a></sup> Its nearly circular orbit led him to a conclusion that it was a planet rather than a comet. Berlin astronomer <a href="/wiki/Johann_Elert_Bode" title="Johann Elert Bode">Johann Elert Bode</a> described Herschel's discovery as "a moving star that can be deemed a hitherto unknown planet-like object circulating beyond the orbit of Saturn".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup> Bode concluded that its near-circular orbit was more like a planet's than a comet's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The object was soon universally accepted as a new planet. By 1783, Herschel acknowledged this to Royal Society president <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Banks" title="Joseph Banks">Joseph Banks</a>: "By the observation of the most eminent Astronomers in Europe it appears that the new star, which I had the honour of pointing out to them in March 1781, is a Primary Planet of our Solar System."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dreyer_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dreyer-42">[37]</a></sup> In recognition of his achievement, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III</a> gave Herschel an annual <a href="/wiki/Stipend" title="Stipend">stipend</a> of £200 on condition that he move to <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Berkshire" title="Windsor, Berkshire">Windsor</a> so that the Royal Family could look through his telescopes (equivalent to £26,000 in 2021).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-UK_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-UK-43">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miner12_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miner12-44">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:27.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Discovery_of_Uranus1781.png" title="Position of Uranus (marked with a cross) on the date of its discovery, the March 13th, 1781"><img alt="Position of Uranus (marked with a cross) on the date of its discovery, the March 13th, 1781" data-file-height="7693" data-file-width="9720" decoding="async" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Discovery_of_Uranus1781.png/120px-Discovery_of_Uranus1781.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Discovery_of_Uranus1781.png/180px-Discovery_of_Uranus1781.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Discovery_of_Uranus1781.png/240px-Discovery_of_Uranus1781.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Position of Uranus (marked with a cross) on the date of its discovery, the March 13th, 1781
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h3>
<p>The name of Uranus references the ancient Greek deity of the sky <a href="/wiki/Uranus_(mythology)" title="Uranus (mythology)">Uranus</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BF%E1%BD%90%CF%81%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%8C%CF%82#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:οὐρανός">Οὐρανός</a></span>), known as <a href="/wiki/Caelus" title="Caelus">Caelus</a> in Roman mythology, the father of <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">Saturn</a>) and grandfather of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>), which was rendered as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Ūranus</i></span> in Latin (<small>IPA: </small><span class="IPA" lang="la-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[ˈuːranʊs]</a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">[2]</a></sup> It is the only planet whose English name is derived directly from a figure of <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>. The adjectival form of Uranus is "Uranian".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[40]</a></sup> The pronunciation of the name <i>Uranus</i> preferred among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomers" title="Astronomers">astronomers</a> is <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/jʊər/: 'ure' in 'cure'">jʊər</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">YOOR</span>-ə-nəs</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCOUP_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBCOUP-1">[1]</a></sup> with stress on the first syllable as in Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Ūranus</i></span>, in contrast to <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'u' in 'push'">ʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">yoo-<span style="font-size:90%">RAY</span>-nəs</i></a>, with stress on the second syllable and a <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length#Traditional_long_and_short_vowels_in_English_orthography" title="Vowel length">long <i>a</i></a>, though both are considered acceptable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[f]</a></sup>
</p><p>Consensus on the name was not reached until almost 70 years after the planet's discovery. During the original discussions following discovery, Maskelyne asked Herschel to "do the astronomical world the faver [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] to give a name to your planet, which is entirely your own, [and] which we are so much obliged to you for the discovery of".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[42]</a></sup> In response to Maskelyne's request, Herschel decided to name the object <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Georgium Sidus</i></span> (George's Star), or the "Georgian Planet" in honour of his new patron, King George III.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[43]</a></sup> He explained this decision in a letter to Joseph Banks:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dreyer_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dreyer-42">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In the fabulous ages of ancient times the appellations of Mercury, Venus, Mars, Jupiter and Saturn were given to the Planets, as being the names of their principal heroes and divinities. In the present more philosophical era it would hardly be allowable to have recourse to the same method and call it Juno, Pallas, Apollo or Minerva, for a name to our new heavenly body. The first consideration of any particular event, or remarkable incident, seems to be its chronology: if in any future age it should be asked, when this last-found Planet was discovered? It would be a very satisfactory answer to say, 'In the reign of King George the Third'.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Herschel's proposed name was not popular outside of Britain and Hanover, and alternatives were soon proposed. Astronomer <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Lalande" title="Jérôme Lalande">Jérôme Lalande</a> proposed that it be named <i>Herschel</i> in honour of its discoverer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francisca_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Francisca-50">[44]</a></sup> Swedish astronomer <a href="/wiki/Erik_Prosperin" title="Erik Prosperin">Erik Prosperin</a> proposed the name <i>Neptune</i>, which was supported by other astronomers who liked the idea to commemorate the victories of the British <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Naval</a> fleet in the course of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> by calling the new planet even <i>Neptune George III</i> or <i>Neptune Great Britain</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lexell_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-lexell-39">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a March 1782 treatise, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Elert_Bode" title="Johann Elert Bode">Bode</a> proposed <i>Uranus</i>, the Latinised version of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek god</a> of the sky, <a href="/wiki/Uranus_(mythology)" title="Uranus (mythology)">Ouranos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bode_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bode-51">[45]</a></sup> Bode argued that the name should follow the mythology so as not to stand out as different from the other planets, and that Uranus was an appropriate name as the father of the first generation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bode_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bode-51">[45]</a></sup> He also noted that elegance of the name in that just as <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">Saturn</a> was the father of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>, the new planet should be named after the father of Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miner12_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miner12-44">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bode_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bode-51">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetsbeyond_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetsbeyond-52">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[47]</a></sup> Bode was however apparently unaware that <i>Uranus</i> was only the Latinised form of the titular deity, and his Roman equivalent was Caelus. In 1789, Bode's <a href="/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences">Royal Academy</a> colleague <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Klaproth" title="Martin Klaproth">Martin Klaproth</a> named his newly discovered element <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> in support of Bode's choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[48]</a></sup> Ultimately, Bode's suggestion became the most widely used, and became universal in 1850 when <a href="/wiki/HM_Nautical_Almanac_Office" title="HM Nautical Almanac Office">HM Nautical Almanac Office</a>, the final holdout, switched from using <i>Georgium Sidus</i> to <i>Uranus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetsbeyond_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-planetsbeyond-52">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranus has two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_symbol" title="Astronomical symbol">astronomical symbols</a>. The first to be proposed, <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_symbol_(fixed_width).svg" title="⛢"><img alt="⛢" data-file-height="15" data-file-width="15" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/16px-Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/24px-Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/32px-Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-symbol_later_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-symbol_later-55">[g]</a></sup> was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_K%C3%B6hler" title="Johann Gottfried Köhler">Johann Gottfried Köhler</a> at Bode's request in 1782.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-platinum_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-platinum-56">[49]</a></sup> Köhler suggested that the new planet be given the symbol for <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, which had been described scientifically only 30 years before. As there was no <a href="/wiki/Alchemical_symbol" title="Alchemical symbol">alchemical symbol</a> for platinum, he suggested <span style="display: inline-block; -ms-transform: rotate(180deg); -webkit-transform: rotate(180deg); transform: rotate(180deg);;">⛢</span> or <span style="display: inline-block; -ms-transform: rotate(90deg); -webkit-transform: rotate(90deg); transform: rotate(90deg);;">⛢</span>, a combination of the planetary-metal symbols ☉ (gold) and ♂ (iron), as platinum (or 'white gold') is found mixed with iron. Bode thought that an upright orientation, ⛢, fit better with the symbols for the other planets while remaining distinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-platinum_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-platinum-56">[49]</a></sup> This symbol predominates in modern astronomical use in the rare cases that symbols are used at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[51]</a></sup> The second symbol, <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_monogram_(fixed_width).svg" title="♅"><img alt="♅" data-file-height="15" data-file-width="15" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg/16px-Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg/24px-Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg/32px-Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-symbol_first_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-symbol_first-59">[h]</a></sup> was suggested by Lalande in 1784. In a letter to Herschel, Lalande described it as "<span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">un globe surmonté par la première lettre de votre nom</i></span>" ("a globe surmounted by the first letter of your surname").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francisca_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Francisca-50">[44]</a></sup> The second symbol is nearly universal in astrology.
</p><p>Uranus is called by a variety of names in other languages. In <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a>, its name is literally translated as the "sky king star" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">天王星</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[55]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>, its official name is <span title="Thai-language text"><i lang="th-Latn">Dao Yurenat</i></span> (<span title="Thai-language text"><span lang="th">ดาวยูเรนัส</span></span>), as in English. Its other name in Thai is <span title="Thai-language text"><i lang="th-Latn">Dao Maritayu</i></span> (<span title="Thai-language text"><span lang="th">ดาวมฤตยู</span></span>, Star of Mṛtyu), after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a> word for 'death', <span title="Sanskrit-language text"><i lang="sa-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Mrtyu" title="Mrtyu">Mrtyu</a></i></span> (<span title="Sanskrit-language text"><span lang="sa">मृत्यु</span></span>). In <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian</a>, its name is <span title="Mongolian-language text"><i lang="mn-Latn">Tengeriin Van</i></span> (<span title="Mongolian-language text"><span lang="mn-Cyrl">Тэнгэрийн ван</span></span>), translated as 'King of the Sky', reflecting its namesake god's role as the ruler of the heavens. In <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_language" title="Hawaiian language">Hawaiian</a>, its name is <span title="Hawaiian-language text"><i lang="haw">Heleʻekala</i></span>, a loanword for the discoverer Herschel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[56]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="Māori language">Māori</a>, its name is <span title="Māori-language text"><i lang="mi">Whērangi</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span></h2>
<p>Uranus orbits the Sun once every 84 years, taking an average of seven years to pass through each of the dozen constellations of the zodiac. In 2033, the planet will have made its third complete orbit around the Sun since being discovered in 1781. The planet has returned to the point of its discovery northeast of <a href="/wiki/Zeta_Tauri" title="Zeta Tauri">Zeta Tauri</a> twice since then, on 25 March 1865 and 29 March 1949. Uranus will return to this location again on 3 April 2033. Its average distance from the Sun is roughly 20 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> (3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a>; 2 billion <a href="/wiki/Mile" title="Mile">mi</a>). The difference between its minimum and maximum distance from the Sun is 1.8 AU, larger than that of any other planet, though not as large as that of dwarf planet <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AA_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-AA-67">[59]</a></sup> The intensity of sunlight varies inversely with the square of distance, and so on Uranus (at about 20 times the distance from the Sun compared to Earth) it is about 1/400 the intensity of light on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The orbital elements of Uranus were first calculated in 1783 by <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">Pierre-Simon Laplace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-georgeforbes_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-georgeforbes-69">[61]</a></sup> With time, discrepancies began to appear between the predicted and observed orbits, and in 1841, <a href="/wiki/John_Couch_Adams" title="John Couch Adams">John Couch Adams</a> first proposed that the differences might be due to the gravitational tug of an unseen planet. In 1845, <a href="/wiki/Urbain_Le_Verrier" title="Urbain Le Verrier">Urbain Le Verrier</a> began his own independent research into Uranus's orbit. On 23 September 1846, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Galle" title="Johann Gottfried Galle">Johann Gottfried Galle</a> located a new planet, later named <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, at nearly the position predicted by Le Verrier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rotational period of the interior of Uranus is 17 hours, 14 minutes. As on all the <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a>, its upper atmosphere experiences strong winds in the direction of rotation. At some latitudes, such as about 60 degrees south, visible features of the atmosphere move much faster, making a full rotation in as little as 14 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Axial_tilt">Axial tilt</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_orientation_1985-2030.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Uranus_orientation_1985-2030.gif/220px-Uranus_orientation_1985-2030.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Uranus_orientation_1985-2030.gif/330px-Uranus_orientation_1985-2030.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Uranus_orientation_1985-2030.gif/440px-Uranus_orientation_1985-2030.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_orientation_1985-2030.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simulated Earth view of Uranus from 1986 to 2030, from southern summer solstice in 1986 to equinox in 2007 and northern summer solstice in 2028.</div></div></div>
<p>The Uranian axis of rotation is approximately parallel with the plane of the Solar System, with an <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> of 97.77° (as defined by prograde rotation). This gives it seasonal changes completely unlike those of the other planets. Near the <a href="/wiki/Solstice" title="Solstice">solstice</a>, one pole faces the Sun continuously and the other faces away, with only a narrow strip around the equator experiencing a rapid day–night cycle, with the Sun low over the horizon. At the other side of Uranus's orbit the orientation of the poles towards the Sun is reversed. Each pole gets around 42 years of continuous sunlight, followed by 42 years of darkness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[64]</a></sup> Near the time of the <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">equinoxes</a>, the Sun faces the equator of Uranus giving a period of day–night cycles similar to those seen on most of the other planets.
</p><p>One result of this axis orientation is that, averaged over the Uranian year, the near-polar regions of Uranus receive a greater energy input from the Sun than its equatorial regions. Nevertheless, Uranus is hotter at its equator than at its poles. The underlying mechanism that causes this is unknown. The reason for Uranus's unusual axial tilt is also not known with certainty, but the usual speculation is that during the formation of the Solar System, an Earth-sized <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">protoplanet</a> collided with Uranus, causing the skewed orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[65]</a></sup> Research by Jacob Kegerreis of <a href="/wiki/Durham_University" title="Durham University">Durham University</a> suggests that the tilt resulted from a rock larger than the Earth crashing into the planet 3 to 4 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[66]</a></sup>
Uranus's south pole was pointed almost directly at the Sun at the time of <i>Voyager 2</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> flyby in 1986. The labelling of this pole as "south" uses the definition currently endorsed by the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>, namely that the north pole of a planet or satellite is the pole that points above the <a href="/wiki/Invariable_plane" title="Invariable plane">invariable plane</a> of the Solar System, regardless of the direction the planet is spinning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[68]</a></sup> A different convention is sometimes used, in which a body's north and south poles are defined according to the <a href="/wiki/Right-hand_rule" title="Right-hand rule">right-hand rule</a> in relation to the direction of rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[69]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visibility">Visibility</span></h3>
<p>The mean <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of Uranus is 5.68 with a standard deviation of 0.17, while the extremes are 5.38 and 6.03.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-22">[18]</a></sup> This range of brightness is near the limit of <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a> visibility. Much of the variability is dependent upon the planetary latitudes being illuminated from the Sun and viewed from the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmude_et_al_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmude_et_al-79">[71]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">angular diameter</a> is between 3.4 and 3.7 arcseconds, compared with 16 to 20 arcseconds for Saturn and 32 to 45 arcseconds for Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ephemeris_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-ephemeris-80">[72]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(astronomy)" title="Opposition (astronomy)">opposition</a>, Uranus is visible to the naked eye in dark skies, and becomes an easy target even in urban conditions with binoculars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-fact-6">[6]</a></sup> In larger amateur telescopes with an objective diameter of between 15 and 23 cm, Uranus appears as a pale cyan disk with distinct <a href="/wiki/Limb_darkening" title="Limb darkening">limb darkening</a>. With a large telescope of 25 cm or wider, cloud patterns, as well as some of the larger satellites, such as <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a>, may be visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus,_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Uranus%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg/220px-Uranus%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Uranus%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg/330px-Uranus%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Uranus%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg/440px-Uranus%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranus,_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Size comparison of Earth and Uranus</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus-intern-en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="794" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Uranus-intern-en.png/220px-Uranus-intern-en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Uranus-intern-en.png/330px-Uranus-intern-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Uranus-intern-en.png/440px-Uranus-intern-en.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranus-intern-en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of the interior of Uranus</div></div></div>
<p>Uranus's mass is roughly 14.5 times that of Earth, making it the least massive of the giant planets. Its diameter is slightly larger than Neptune's at roughly four times that of Earth. A resulting density of 1.27 g/cm<sup>3</sup> makes Uranus the second least dense planet, after Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seidelmann_Archinal_A'hearn_et_al._2007_12-7"><a href="#cite_note-Seidelmann_Archinal_A'hearn_et_al._2007-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_Campbell_et_al._1992_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson_Campbell_et_al._1992-15">[12]</a></sup> This value indicates that it is made primarily of various ices, such as water, ammonia, and methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup> The total mass of ice in Uranus's interior is not precisely known, because different figures emerge depending on the model chosen; it must be between 9.3 and 13.5 Earth masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-82">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">Hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> constitute only a small part of the total, with between 0.5 and 1.5 Earth masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup> The remainder of the non-ice mass (0.5 to 3.7 Earth masses) is accounted for by <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocky material</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The standard model of Uranus's structure is that it consists of three layers: a rocky (<a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a>/<a href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93nickel_alloy" title="Iron–nickel alloy">iron–nickel</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_(geology)" title="Core (geology)">core</a> in the centre, an icy <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> in the middle and an outer gaseous hydrogen/helium envelope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-6"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faure2007_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faure2007-83">[75]</a></sup> The core is relatively small, with a mass of only 0.55 Earth masses and a radius less than 20% of Uranus'; the mantle comprises its bulk, with around 13.4 Earth masses, and the upper atmosphere is relatively insubstantial, weighing about 0.5 Earth masses and extending for the last 20% of Uranus's radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-7"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faure2007_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faure2007-83">[75]</a></sup> Uranus's core <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> is around 9 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, with a <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> in the centre of 8 million <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bars</a> (800 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigapascal" title="Gigapascal">GPa</a>) and a temperature of about 5000 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-82">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faure2007_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Faure2007-83">[75]</a></sup> The ice mantle is not in fact composed of ice in the conventional sense, but of a hot and dense fluid consisting of water, ammonia and other <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-8"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faure2007_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-Faure2007-83">[75]</a></sup> This fluid, which has a high electrical conductivity, is sometimes called a water–ammonia ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atreya2006_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atreya2006-84">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The extreme pressure and temperature deep within Uranus may break up the methane molecules, with the carbon atoms condensing into crystals of diamond that rain down through the mantle like hailstones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP-20170825_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-20170825-86">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[79]</a></sup> Very-high-pressure experiments at the <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Livermore_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory">Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory</a> suggest that the base of the mantle may comprise an ocean of metallic liquid carbon, perhaps with floating solid 'diamond-bergs'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eggert_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eggert-88">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bland,_Eric_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bland,_Eric-89">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[82]</a></sup> Scientists also believe that <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rainfalls</a> of solid <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamonds</a> occur on Uranus, as well as on <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The bulk compositions of Uranus and Neptune are different from those of Jupiter and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, with ice dominating over gases, hence justifying their separate classification as <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a>. There may be a layer of ionic water where the water molecules break down into a soup of hydrogen and oxygen ions, and deeper down <a href="/wiki/Superionic_water" title="Superionic water">superionic water</a> in which the oxygen crystallises but the hydrogen ions move freely within the oxygen lattice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the model considered above is reasonably standard, it is not unique; other models also satisfy observations. For instance, if substantial amounts of hydrogen and rocky material are mixed in the ice mantle, the total mass of ices in the interior will be lower, and, correspondingly, the total mass of rocks and hydrogen will be higher. Presently available data does not allow a scientific determination of which model is correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Podolak_et_al._2000-82">[74]</a></sup> The fluid interior structure of Uranus means that it has no solid surface. The gaseous atmosphere gradually transitions into the internal liquid layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-9"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup> For the sake of convenience, a revolving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate spheroid</a> set at the point at which atmospheric pressure equals 1 bar (100 kPa) is conditionally designated as a "surface". It has equatorial and <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">polar</a> radii of 25,559 ± 4 km (15,881.6 ± 2.5 mi) and 24,973 ± 20 km (15,518 ± 12 mi), respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seidelmann_Archinal_A'hearn_et_al._2007_12-8"><a href="#cite_note-Seidelmann_Archinal_A'hearn_et_al._2007-12">[11]</a></sup> This surface is used throughout this article as a zero point for altitudes.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_heat">Internal heat</span></h4>
<p>Uranus's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_heat" title="Internal heat">internal heat</a> appears markedly lower than that of the other giant planets; in astronomical terms, it has a low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_flux" title="Thermal flux">thermal flux</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanel_Conrath_et_al._1986_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hanel_Conrath_et_al._1986-94">[86]</a></sup> Why Uranus's internal temperature is so low is still not understood. Neptune, which is Uranus's near twin in size and composition, radiates 2.61 times as much energy into space as it receives from the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup> but Uranus radiates hardly any excess heat at all. The total power radiated by Uranus in the <a href="/wiki/Far_infrared" title="Far infrared">far infrared</a> (i.e. heat) part of the spectrum is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000106000000000000♠"></span>1.06<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.08</span> times the solar energy absorbed in its <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990-95">[87]</a></sup> Uranus's heat flux is only <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6998420000000000000♠"></span>0.042<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.047 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">W</a>/<a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">m<sup>2</sup></a></span>, which is lower than the internal heat flux of Earth of about <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6998750000000000000♠"></span>0.075 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">W</a>/<a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">m<sup>2</sup></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990-95">[87]</a></sup> The lowest temperature recorded in Uranus's <a href="/wiki/Tropopause" title="Tropopause">tropopause</a> is 49 K (−224.2 °C; −371.5 °F), making Uranus the coldest planet in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990-95">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the hypotheses for this discrepancy suggests that when Uranus was hit by a supermassive impactor, which caused it to expel most of its primordial heat, it was left with a depleted core temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[88]</a></sup> This impact hypothesis is also used in some attempts to explain the planet's axial tilt. Another hypothesis is that some form of barrier exists in Uranus's upper layers that prevents the core's heat from reaching the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-10"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> may take place in a set of compositionally different layers, which may inhibit the upward <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_conduction" title="Heat conduction">heat transport</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990-95">[87]</a></sup> perhaps <a href="/wiki/Double_diffusive_convection" title="Double diffusive convection">double diffusive convection</a> is a limiting factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_20-11"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-20">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a recent study, the ice giants' interior conditions were mimicked by compressing water containing minerals like olivine and ferropericlase. It showed that much magnesium could be dissolved in the liquid interiors of Uranus and Neptune. A thermal insulation layer made of dissolved magnesium in Uranus due to a larger quantity in Uranus than Neptune was proposed as a possible explanation of Uranus's low temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Uranus" title="Atmosphere of Uranus">Atmosphere of Uranus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adding_to_Uranus%27s_legacy.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1086" data-file-width="1022" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Adding_to_Uranus%27s_legacy.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Adding_to_Uranus%27s_legacy.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Adding_to_Uranus%27s_legacy.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Adding_to_Uranus%27s_legacy.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Adding_to_Uranus%27s_legacy.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Adding_to_Uranus%27s_legacy.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adding_to_Uranus%27s_legacy.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uranus's atmosphere taken during the Outer Planet Atmosphere Legacy (OPAL) program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[90]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Although there is no well-defined solid surface within Uranus's interior, the outermost part of Uranus's gaseous envelope that is accessible to remote sensing is called its <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-7"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup> Remote-sensing capability extends down to roughly 300 km below the 1 bar (100 kPa) level, with a corresponding pressure around 100 bar (10 MPa) and temperature of 320 K (47 °C; 116 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Pater_Romani_et_al._1991_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_Pater_Romani_et_al._1991-99">[91]</a></sup> The tenuous <a href="/wiki/Thermosphere" title="Thermosphere">thermosphere</a> extends over two planetary radii from the nominal surface, which is defined to lie at a pressure of 1 bar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_Sandel_et_al._1987_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_Sandel_et_al._1987-100">[92]</a></sup> The Uranian atmosphere can be divided into three layers: the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>, between altitudes of −300 and 50 km (−186 and 31 mi) and pressures from 100 to 0.1 bar (10 MPa to 10 kPa); the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>, spanning altitudes between 50 and 4,000 km (31 and 2,485 mi) and pressures of between <span class="nowrap">0.1 and 10<sup>−10</sup> bar</span> (10 kPa to 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micropascal" title="Micropascal">µPa</a>); and the thermosphere extending from 4,000 km to as high as 50,000 km from the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-8"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup> There is no <a href="/wiki/Mesosphere" title="Mesosphere">mesosphere</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span></h4>
<p>The composition of Uranus's atmosphere is different from its bulk, consisting mainly of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_hydrogen" title="Molecular hydrogen">molecular hydrogen</a> and helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-9"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup> The helium <a href="/wiki/Gas_composition" title="Gas composition">molar fraction</a>, i.e. the number of helium <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> per molecule of gas, is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6999150000000000000♠"></span>0.15<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.03</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conrath_Gautier_et_al._1987_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Conrath_Gautier_et_al._1987-24">[20]</a></sup> in the upper troposphere, which corresponds to a mass fraction <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6999260000000000000♠"></span>0.26<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.05</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-10"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990_95-4"><a href="#cite_note-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990-95">[87]</a></sup> This value is close to the protosolar helium mass fraction of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6999275000000000000♠"></span>0.275<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.01</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lodders_2003_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lodders_2003-101">[93]</a></sup> indicating that helium has not settled in its centre as it has in the gas giants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-11"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup> The third-most-abundant component of Uranus's atmosphere is methane (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1060974363">.mw-parser-output .template-chem2-su{display:inline-block;font-size:80%;line-height:1;vertical-align:-0.35em}.mw-parser-output .template-chem2-su>span{display:block}.mw-parser-output sub.template-chem2-sub{font-size:80%;vertical-align:-0.35em}.mw-parser-output sup.template-chem2-sup{font-size:80%;vertical-align:0.65em}</style><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<sub class="template-chem2-sub">4</sub></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-12"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup> Methane has prominent <a href="/wiki/Absorption_band" title="Absorption band">absorption bands</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-infrared" title="Near-infrared">near-infrared</a> (IR), making Uranus <a href="/wiki/Aquamarine_(color)" title="Aquamarine (color)">aquamarine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cyan" title="Cyan">cyan</a> in colour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-13"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup> Methane molecules account for 2.3% of the atmosphere by molar fraction below the methane cloud deck at the pressure level of 1.3 bar (130 kPa); this represents about 20 to 30 times the carbon abundance found in the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-14"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindal_Lyons_et_al._1987_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lindal_Lyons_et_al._1987-23">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler_1986_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler_1986-102">[94]</a></sup> The mixing ratio<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[i]</a></sup> is much lower in the upper atmosphere due to its extremely low temperature, which lowers the saturation level and causes excess methane to freeze out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bishop_Atreya_et_al._1990_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bishop_Atreya_et_al._1990-104">[95]</a></sup> The abundances of less volatile compounds such as ammonia, water, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> in the deep atmosphere are poorly known. They are probably also higher than solar values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-15"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Pater_Romani_et_al._1989_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_Pater_Romani_et_al._1989-105">[96]</a></sup> Along with methane, trace amounts of various <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> are found in the stratosphere of Uranus, which are thought to be produced from methane by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">photolysis</a> induced by the solar <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> (UV) radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Summers_&amp;_Strobel_1989_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Summers_&amp;_Strobel_1989-106">[97]</a></sup> They include <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">C<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">6</sub></span>), <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">C<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methylacetylene" title="Methylacetylene">methylacetylene</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub>C<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>H</span>), and <a href="/wiki/Diacetylene" title="Diacetylene">diacetylene</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">C<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>HC<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>H</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bishop_Atreya_et_al._1990_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bishop_Atreya_et_al._1990-104">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgdorf_Orton_et_al._2006_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burgdorf_Orton_et_al._2006-107">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encrenaz_2003_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encrenaz_2003-108">[99]</a></sup> Spectroscopy has also uncovered traces of water vapour, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> in the upper atmosphere, which can only originate from an external source such as infalling dust and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgdorf_Orton_et_al._2006_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burgdorf_Orton_et_al._2006-107">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encrenaz_2003_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encrenaz_2003-108">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encrenaz_Lellouch_et_al._2004_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encrenaz_Lellouch_et_al._2004-109">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Troposphere">Troposphere</span></h4>
<p>The troposphere is the lowest and densest part of the atmosphere and is characterised by a decrease in temperature with altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-16"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup> The temperature falls from about 320 K (47 °C; 116 °F) at the base of the nominal troposphere at −300 km to 53 K (−220 °C; −364 °F) at 50 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Pater_Romani_et_al._1991_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-de_Pater_Romani_et_al._1991-99">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler_1986_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler_1986-102">[94]</a></sup> The temperatures in the coldest upper region of the troposphere (the <a href="/wiki/Tropopause" title="Tropopause">tropopause</a>) actually vary in the range between 49 and 57 K (−224 and −216 °C; −371 and −357 °F) depending on planetary latitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-17"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanel_Conrath_et_al._1986_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hanel_Conrath_et_al._1986-94">[86]</a></sup> The tropopause region is responsible for the vast majority of Uranus's thermal <a href="/wiki/Far_infrared" title="Far infrared">far infrared</a> emissions, thus determining its <a href="/wiki/Effective_temperature" title="Effective temperature">effective temperature</a> of 59.1 ± 0.3 K (−214.1 ± 0.3 °C; −353.3 ± 0.5 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanel_Conrath_et_al._1986_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hanel_Conrath_et_al._1986-94">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990_95-5"><a href="#cite_note-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990-95">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>The troposphere is thought to have a highly complex cloud structure; water clouds are hypothesised to lie in the pressure range of 50 to 100 bar (5 to 10 MPa), <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a> clouds in the range of 20 to 40 bar (2 to 4 MPa), ammonia or <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> clouds at between 3 and 10 bar (0.3 and 1 MPa) and finally directly detected thin methane clouds at 1 to 2 bar (0.1 to 0.2 MPa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-18"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindal_Lyons_et_al._1987_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lindal_Lyons_et_al._1987-23">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Pater_Romani_et_al._1991_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-de_Pater_Romani_et_al._1991-99">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atreya_Wong_2005_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atreya_Wong_2005-110">[101]</a></sup> The troposphere is a dynamic part of the atmosphere, exhibiting strong winds, bright clouds and seasonal changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_atmosphere">Upper atmosphere</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alien_aurorae_on_Uranus_(remastered).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1083" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Alien_aurorae_on_Uranus_%28remastered%29.jpg/220px-Alien_aurorae_on_Uranus_%28remastered%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Alien_aurorae_on_Uranus_%28remastered%29.jpg/330px-Alien_aurorae_on_Uranus_%28remastered%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Alien_aurorae_on_Uranus_%28remastered%29.jpg/440px-Alien_aurorae_on_Uranus_%28remastered%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alien_aurorae_on_Uranus_(remastered).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aurorae on Uranus taken by the Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph (STIS) installed on <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[102]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The middle layer of the Uranian atmosphere is the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>, where temperature generally increases with altitude from 53 K (−220 °C; −364 °F) in the <a href="/wiki/Tropopause" title="Tropopause">tropopause</a> to between 800 and 850 K (527 and 577 °C; 980 and 1,070 °F) at the base of the thermosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_Sandel_et_al._1987_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_Sandel_et_al._1987-100">[92]</a></sup> The heating of the stratosphere is caused by absorption of solar UV and IR radiation by methane and other <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_et_al._2001_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young_et_al._2001-112">[103]</a></sup> which form in this part of the atmosphere as a result of methane <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">photolysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Summers_&amp;_Strobel_1989_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Summers_&amp;_Strobel_1989-106">[97]</a></sup> Heat is also conducted from the hot thermosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_et_al._2001_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Young_et_al._2001-112">[103]</a></sup> The hydrocarbons occupy a relatively narrow layer at altitudes of between 100 and 300 km corresponding to a pressure range of 1000 to 10 Pa and temperatures of between 75 and 170 K (−198 and −103 °C; −325 and −154 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bishop_Atreya_et_al._1990_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bishop_Atreya_et_al._1990-104">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgdorf_Orton_et_al._2006_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-Burgdorf_Orton_et_al._2006-107">[98]</a></sup> The most abundant hydrocarbons are methane, <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> with <a href="/wiki/Mixing_ratio" title="Mixing ratio">mixing ratios</a> of around 10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="2999300000000000000♠"></span>−7</span></sup> relative to hydrogen. The mixing ratio of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> is similar at these altitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bishop_Atreya_et_al._1990_104-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bishop_Atreya_et_al._1990-104">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgdorf_Orton_et_al._2006_107-3"><a href="#cite_note-Burgdorf_Orton_et_al._2006-107">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encrenaz_Lellouch_et_al._2004_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encrenaz_Lellouch_et_al._2004-109">[100]</a></sup> Heavier hydrocarbons and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> have mixing ratios three orders of magnitude lower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgdorf_Orton_et_al._2006_107-4"><a href="#cite_note-Burgdorf_Orton_et_al._2006-107">[98]</a></sup> The abundance ratio of water is around 7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="2999100000000000000♠"></span>−9</span></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encrenaz_2003_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-Encrenaz_2003-108">[99]</a></sup> Ethane and acetylene tend to condense in the colder lower part of stratosphere and tropopause (below 10 mBar level) forming haze layers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Summers_&amp;_Strobel_1989_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Summers_&amp;_Strobel_1989-106">[97]</a></sup> which may be partly responsible for the bland appearance of Uranus. The concentration of hydrocarbons in the Uranian stratosphere above the haze is significantly lower than in the stratospheres of the other giant planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bishop_Atreya_et_al._1990_104-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bishop_Atreya_et_al._1990-104">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999-113">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>The outermost layer of the Uranian atmosphere is the thermosphere and corona, which has a uniform temperature around 800 to 850 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-19"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999-113">[104]</a></sup> The heat sources necessary to sustain such a high level are not understood, as neither the solar UV nor the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">auroral</a> activity can provide the necessary energy to maintain these temperatures. The weak cooling efficiency due to the lack of hydrocarbons in the stratosphere above 0.1 mBar pressure level may contribute too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_Sandel_et_al._1987_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_Sandel_et_al._1987-100">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999-113">[104]</a></sup> In addition to molecular hydrogen, the thermosphere-corona contains many free hydrogen atoms. Their small mass and high temperatures explain why the corona extends as far as 50,000 km (31,000 mi), or two Uranian radii, from its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_Sandel_et_al._1987_100-3"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_Sandel_et_al._1987-100">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999_113-3"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999-113">[104]</a></sup> This extended corona is a unique feature of Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999_113-4"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999-113">[104]</a></sup> Its effects include a <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a> on small particles orbiting Uranus, causing a general depletion of dust in the Uranian rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_Sandel_et_al._1987_100-4"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_Sandel_et_al._1987-100">[92]</a></sup> The Uranian thermosphere, together with the upper part of the stratosphere, corresponds to the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> of Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler_1986_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler_1986-102">[94]</a></sup> Observations show that the ionosphere occupies altitudes from 2,000 to 10,000 km (1,200 to 6,200 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler_1986_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler_1986-102">[94]</a></sup> The Uranian ionosphere is denser than that of either Saturn or Neptune, which may arise from the low concentration of hydrocarbons in the stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999_113-5"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999-113">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trafton_Miller_et_al._1999_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trafton_Miller_et_al._1999-114">[105]</a></sup> The ionosphere is mainly sustained by solar UV radiation and its density depends on the <a href="/wiki/Space_weather" title="Space weather">solar activity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encrenaz_Drossart_et_al._2003_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encrenaz_Drossart_et_al._2003-115">[106]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auroral" title="Auroral">Auroral</a> activity is insignificant as compared to Jupiter and Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999_113-6"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999-113">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lam_Miller_et_al._1997_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lam_Miller_et_al._1997-116">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetosphere">Magnetosphere</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA23683-Uranus-MagneticField-20200325.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="585" data-file-width="1040" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/PIA23683-Uranus-MagneticField-20200325.gif/250px-PIA23683-Uranus-MagneticField-20200325.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/PIA23683-Uranus-MagneticField-20200325.gif/375px-PIA23683-Uranus-MagneticField-20200325.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/PIA23683-Uranus-MagneticField-20200325.gif/500px-PIA23683-Uranus-MagneticField-20200325.gif 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA23683-Uranus-MagneticField-20200325.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The magnetic field of Uranus<br/>(animated; 25 March 2020)</div></div></div><p>Before the arrival of <i>Voyager 2</i>, no measurements of the Uranian <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> had been taken, so its nature remained a mystery. Before 1986, scientists had expected the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> of Uranus to be in line with the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, because it would then align with Uranus's poles that lie in the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-117">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Voyager</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s observations revealed that Uranus's magnetic field is peculiar, both because it does not originate from its geometric centre, and because it is tilted at 59° from the axis of rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-117">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell993_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell993-118">[109]</a></sup> In fact the magnetic dipole is shifted from Uranus's centre towards the south rotational pole by as much as one third of the planetary radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-117">[108]</a></sup> This unusual geometry
