results in a highly asymmetric magnetosphere, where the magnetic field strength on the surface in the southern hemisphere can be as low as 0.1 <a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss</a> (10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microtesla" title="Microtesla">µT</a>), whereas in the northern hemisphere it can be as high as 1.1 gauss (110 µT).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_117-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-117">[108]</a></sup> The average field at the surface is 0.23 gauss (23 µT).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_117-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-117">[108]</a></sup> Studies of <i>Voyager 2</i> data in 2017 suggest that this asymmetry causes Uranus's magnetosphere to connect with the solar wind once a Uranian day, opening the planet to the Sun's particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[110]</a></sup> In comparison, the magnetic field of Earth is roughly as strong at either pole, and its "magnetic equator" is roughly parallel with its geographical equator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell993_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Russell993-118">[109]</a></sup> The dipole moment of Uranus is 50 times that of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_117-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-117">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell993_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-Russell993-118">[109]</a></sup> Neptune has a similarly displaced and tilted magnetic field, suggesting that this may be a common feature of ice giants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell993_118-3"><a href="#cite_note-Russell993-118">[109]</a></sup> One hypothesis is that, unlike the magnetic fields of the terrestrial and gas giants, which are generated within their cores, the ice giants' magnetic fields are generated by motion at relatively shallow depths, for instance, in the water–ammonia ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atreya2006_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atreya2006-84">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[111]</a></sup> Another possible explanation for the magnetosphere's alignment is that there are oceans of liquid diamond in Uranus's interior that would deter the magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bland,_Eric_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bland,_Eric-89">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite its curious alignment, in other respects the Uranian magnetosphere is like those of other planets: it has a <a href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">bow shock</a> at about 23 Uranian radii ahead of it, a <a href="/wiki/Magnetopause" title="Magnetopause">magnetopause</a> at 18 Uranian radii, a fully developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetotail" title="Magnetotail">magnetotail</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation_belt" title="Radiation belt">radiation belts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_117-6"><a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-117">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell993_118-4"><a href="#cite_note-Russell993-118">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986-121">[112]</a></sup> Overall, the structure of Uranus's magnetosphere is different from Jupiter's and more similar to Saturn's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_117-7"><a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-117">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell993_118-5"><a href="#cite_note-Russell993-118">[109]</a></sup> Uranus's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetotail" title="Magnetotail">magnetotail</a> trails behind it into space for millions of kilometres and is twisted by its sideways rotation into a long corkscrew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_117-8"><a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-117">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranus's magnetosphere contains <a href="/wiki/Charged_particle" title="Charged particle">charged particles</a>: mainly <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, with a small amount of <a href="/wiki/Dihydrogen_cation" title="Dihydrogen cation">H<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup></a> ions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell993_118-6"><a href="#cite_note-Russell993-118">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986-121">[112]</a></sup> Many of these particles probably derive from the thermosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986-121">[112]</a></sup> The ion and electron energies can be as high as 4 and 1.2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megaelectronvolt" title="Megaelectronvolt">megaelectronvolts</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986_121-3"><a href="#cite_note-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986-121">[112]</a></sup> The density of low-energy (below 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiloelectronvolt" title="Kiloelectronvolt">kiloelectronvolt</a>) ions in the inner magnetosphere is about 2 cm<sup>−3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bridge1986_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bridge1986-123">[114]</a></sup> The particle population is strongly affected by the Uranian moons, which sweep through the magnetosphere, leaving noticeable gaps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986_121-4"><a href="#cite_note-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986-121">[112]</a></sup> The particle <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">flux</a> is high enough to cause darkening or <a href="/wiki/Space_weathering" title="Space weathering">space weathering</a> of their surfaces on an astronomically rapid timescale of 100,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986_121-5"><a href="#cite_note-Krimigis_Armstrong_et_al._1986-121">[112]</a></sup> This may be the cause of the uniformly dark colouration of the Uranian satellites and rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-summary_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-summary-124">[115]</a></sup> Uranus has relatively well developed aurorae, which are seen as bright arcs around both magnetic poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999_113-7"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999-113">[104]</a></sup> Unlike Jupiter's, Uranus's aurorae seem to be insignificant for the energy balance of the planetary thermosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lam_Miller_et_al._1997_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lam_Miller_et_al._1997-116">[107]</a></sup> In March 2020, NASA astronomers reported the detection of a large atmospheric magnetic bubble, also known as a <a href="/wiki/Plasmoid" title="Plasmoid">plasmoid</a>, released into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> from the planet Uranus, after reevaluating old data recorded by the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> during a flyby of the planet in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20200325_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20200325-125">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20200327_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20200327-126">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Uranus" title="Climate of Uranus">Climate of Uranus</a></div>
<p>At ultraviolet and visible wavelengths, Uranus's atmosphere is bland in comparison to the other giant planets, even to Neptune, which it otherwise closely resembles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup> When <i>Voyager 2</i> flew by Uranus in 1986, it observed a total of ten <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">cloud</a> features across the entire planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetary-127">[118]</a></sup> One proposed explanation for this dearth of features is that Uranus's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_heat" title="Internal heat">internal heat</a> is markedly lower than that of the other giant planets, as stated previously Uranus is the coldest planet in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_21-20"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990_95-6"><a href="#cite_note-Pearl_Conrath_et_al._1990-95">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Banded_structure.2C_winds_and_clouds"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Banded_structure,_winds_and_clouds">Banded structure, winds and clouds</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_Dark_spot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Uranus_Dark_spot.jpg/220px-Uranus_Dark_spot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Uranus_Dark_spot.jpg/330px-Uranus_Dark_spot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Uranus_Dark_spot.jpg/440px-Uranus_Dark_spot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_Dark_spot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first dark spot observed on Uranus. Image obtained by the HST <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Camera_for_Surveys" title="Advanced Camera for Surveys">ACS</a> in 2006.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1986, <i>Voyager 2</i> found that the visible southern hemisphere of Uranus can be subdivided into two regions: a bright polar cap and dark equatorial bands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup> Their boundary is located at about −45° of <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>. A narrow band straddling the latitudinal range from −45 to −50° is the brightest large feature on its visible surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005-128">[119]</a></sup> It is called a southern "collar". The cap and collar are thought to be a dense region of methane clouds located within the pressure range of 1.3 to 2 bar (see above).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rages_Hammel_et_al._2004_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rages_Hammel_et_al._2004-129">[120]</a></sup> Besides the large-scale banded structure, <i>Voyager 2</i> observed ten small bright clouds, most lying several degrees to the north from the collar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup> In all other respects Uranus looked like a dynamically dead planet in 1986. <i>Voyager 2</i> arrived during the height of Uranus's southern summer and could not observe the northern hemisphere. At the beginning of the 21st century, when the northern polar region came into view, the Hubble Space Telescope (HST) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keck_telescopes" title="Keck telescopes">Keck</a> telescope initially observed neither a collar nor a polar cap in the northern hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005-128">[119]</a></sup> So Uranus appeared to be asymmetric: bright near the south pole and uniformly dark in the region north of the southern collar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005-128">[119]</a></sup> In 2007, when Uranus passed its equinox, the southern collar almost disappeared, and a faint northern collar emerged near 45° of <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_Fry_et_al._2009_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_Fry_et_al._2009-130">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1990s, the number of the observed bright cloud features grew considerably partly because new high-resolution imaging techniques became available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup> Most were found in the northern hemisphere as it started to become visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup> An early explanation—that bright clouds are easier to identify in its dark part, whereas in the southern hemisphere the bright collar masks them – was shown to be incorrect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka_('Uranus')_2001_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka_('Uranus')_2001-131">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2004,_2005_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2004,_2005-132">[123]</a></sup> Nevertheless, there are differences between the clouds of each hemisphere. The northern clouds are smaller, sharper and brighter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2004,_2005_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2004,_2005-132">[123]</a></sup> They appear to lie at a higher altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2004,_2005_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2004,_2005-132">[123]</a></sup> The lifetime of clouds spans several orders of magnitude. Some small clouds live for hours; at least one southern cloud may have persisted since the <i>Voyager 2</i> flyby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-planetary-127">[118]</a></sup> Recent observation also discovered that cloud features on Uranus have a lot in common with those on Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup> For example, the dark spots common on Neptune had never been observed on Uranus before 2006, when the first such feature dubbed <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Uranus#Uranus_Dark_Spot" title="Climate of Uranus">Uranus Dark Spot</a> was imaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkSpot_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-DarkSpot-133">[124]</a></sup> The speculation is that Uranus is becoming more Neptune-like during its equinoctial season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel2007_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel2007-134">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>The tracking of numerous cloud features allowed determination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zonal_and_meridional" title="Zonal and meridional">zonal</a> winds blowing in the upper troposphere of Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-9"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup> At the equator winds are retrograde, which means that they blow in the reverse direction to the planetary rotation. Their speeds are from −360 to −180 km/h (−220 to −110 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-10"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005-128">[119]</a></sup> Wind speeds increase with the distance from the equator, reaching zero values near ±20° latitude, where the troposphere's temperature minimum is located.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-11"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanel_Conrath_et_al._1986_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hanel_Conrath_et_al._1986-94">[86]</a></sup> Closer to the poles, the winds shift to a prograde direction, flowing with Uranus's rotation. Wind speeds continue to increase reaching maxima at ±60° latitude before falling to zero at the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-12"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup> Wind speeds at −40° latitude range from 540 to 720 km/h (340 to 450 mph). Because the collar obscures all clouds below that parallel, speeds between it and the southern pole are impossible to measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-13"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup> In contrast, in the northern hemisphere maximum speeds as high as 860 km/h (540 mph) are observed near +50° latitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_28-14"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005_128-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005-128">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_Rages_et_al._2001_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_Rages_et_al._2001-135">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seasonal_variation">Seasonal variation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_clouds.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="383" data-file-width="260" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Uranus_clouds.jpg/170px-Uranus_clouds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Uranus_clouds.jpg/255px-Uranus_clouds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/Uranus_clouds.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_clouds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uranus in 2005. Rings, southern collar and a bright cloud in the northern hemisphere are visible (HST ACS image).</div></div></div>
<p>For a short period from March to May 2004, large clouds appeared in the Uranian atmosphere, giving it a Neptune-like appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2004,_2005_132-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2004,_2005-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[127]</a></sup> Observations included record-breaking wind speeds of 820 km/h (510 mph) and a persistent thunderstorm referred to as "Fourth of July fireworks".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-planetary-127">[118]</a></sup> On 23 August 2006, researchers at the Space Science Institute (Boulder, Colorado) and the University of Wisconsin observed a dark spot on Uranus's surface, giving scientists more insight into Uranus atmospheric activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkSpot_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-DarkSpot-133">[124]</a></sup> Why this sudden upsurge in activity occurred is not fully known, but it appears that Uranus's extreme axial tilt results in extreme seasonal variations in its weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weather_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-weather-137">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel2007_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel2007-134">[125]</a></sup> Determining the nature of this seasonal variation is difficult because good data on Uranus's atmosphere has existed for less than 84 years, or one full Uranian year. <a href="/wiki/Photometry_(astronomy)" title="Photometry (astronomy)">Photometry</a> over the course of half a Uranian year (beginning in the 1950s) has shown regular variation in the brightness in two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_band" title="Spectral band">spectral bands</a>, with maxima occurring at the solstices and minima occurring at the equinoxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lockwood_&amp;_Jerzykiewicz_2006_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lockwood_&amp;_Jerzykiewicz_2006-138">[129]</a></sup> A similar periodic variation, with maxima at the solstices, has been noted in <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> measurements of the deep troposphere begun in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klein_&amp;_Hofstadter_2006_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klein_&amp;_Hofstadter_2006-139">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">Stratospheric</a> temperature measurements beginning in the 1970s also showed maximum values near the 1986 solstice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_et_al._2001_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-Young_et_al._2001-112">[103]</a></sup> The majority of this variability is thought to occur owing to changes in the viewing geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka_('Uranus')_2001_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka_('Uranus')_2001-131">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are some indications that physical seasonal changes are happening in Uranus. Although Uranus is known to have a bright south polar region, the north pole is fairly dim, which is incompatible with the model of the seasonal change outlined above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel2007_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel2007-134">[125]</a></sup> During its previous northern solstice in 1944, Uranus displayed elevated levels of brightness, which suggests that the north pole was not always so dim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lockwood_&amp;_Jerzykiewicz_2006_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lockwood_&amp;_Jerzykiewicz_2006-138">[129]</a></sup> This information implies that the visible pole brightens some time before the solstice and darkens after the equinox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel2007_134-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel2007-134">[125]</a></sup> Detailed analysis of the visible and microwave data revealed that the periodical changes of brightness are not completely symmetrical around the solstices, which also indicates a change in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meridional" title="Meridional">meridional</a> albedo patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel2007_134-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel2007-134">[125]</a></sup> In the 1990s, as Uranus moved away from its solstice, Hubble and ground-based telescopes revealed that the south polar cap darkened noticeably (except the southern collar, which remained bright),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rages_Hammel_et_al._2004_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rages_Hammel_et_al._2004-129">[120]</a></sup> whereas the northern hemisphere demonstrated increasing activity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary_127-3"><a href="#cite_note-planetary-127">[118]</a></sup> such as cloud formations and stronger winds, bolstering expectations that it should brighten soon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2004,_2005_132-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2004,_2005-132">[123]</a></sup> This indeed happened in 2007 when it passed an equinox: a faint northern polar collar arose, and the southern collar became nearly invisible, although the zonal wind profile remained slightly asymmetric, with northern winds being somewhat slower than southern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_Fry_et_al._2009_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_Fry_et_al._2009-130">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>The mechanism of these physical changes is still not clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel2007_134-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel2007-134">[125]</a></sup> Near the summer and winter solstices, Uranus's hemispheres lie alternately either in full glare of the Sun's rays or facing deep space. The brightening of the sunlit hemisphere is thought to result from the local thickening of the methane clouds and haze layers located in the troposphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rages_Hammel_et_al._2004_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rages_Hammel_et_al._2004-129">[120]</a></sup> The bright collar at −45° latitude is also connected with methane clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rages_Hammel_et_al._2004_129-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rages_Hammel_et_al._2004-129">[120]</a></sup> Other changes in the southern polar region can be explained by changes in the lower cloud layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rages_Hammel_et_al._2004_129-4"><a href="#cite_note-Rages_Hammel_et_al._2004-129">[120]</a></sup> The variation of the microwave <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emission_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Emission (electromagnetic radiation)">emission</a> from Uranus is probably caused by changes in the deep tropospheric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circulation_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Circulation (fluid dynamics)">circulation</a>, because thick polar clouds and haze may inhibit convection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hofstadter_&amp;_Butler_2003_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hofstadter_&amp;_Butler_2003-140">[131]</a></sup> Now that the spring and autumn equinoxes are arriving on Uranus, the dynamics are changing and convection can occur again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary_127-4"><a href="#cite_note-planetary-127">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hofstadter_&amp;_Butler_2003_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hofstadter_&amp;_Butler_2003-140">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Formation and evolution of the Solar System</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For details of the evolution of Uranus's orbit, see <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">Nice model</a>.</div>
<p>It is argued that the differences between the ice giants and the gas giants arise from their formation history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thommes1999_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thommes1999-141">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brunini1999_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brunini1999-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dangelo2021_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-dangelo2021-143">[134]</a></sup> The Solar System is hypothesised to have formed from a rotating disk of gas and dust known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presolar_nebula" title="Presolar nebula">presolar nebula</a>. Much of the nebula's gas, primarily hydrogen and helium, formed the Sun, and the dust grains collected together to form the first protoplanets. As the planets grew, some of them eventually accreted enough matter for their gravity to hold on to the nebula's leftover gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thommes1999_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thommes1999-141">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brunini1999_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brunini1999-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dangelo_bodenheimer_2013_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-dangelo_bodenheimer_2013-144">[135]</a></sup> The more gas they held onto, the larger they became; the larger they became, the more gas they held onto until a critical point was reached, and their size began to increase exponentially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dl2018_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-dl2018-145">[136]</a></sup> The ice giants, with only a few Earth masses of nebular gas, never reached that critical point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thommes1999_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thommes1999-141">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brunini1999_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brunini1999-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard_Jewitt_Kleyna_2006_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard_Jewitt_Kleyna_2006-146">[137]</a></sup> Recent simulations of <a href="/wiki/Planetary_migration" title="Planetary migration">planetary migration</a> have suggested that both ice giants formed closer to the Sun than their present positions, and moved outwards after formation (the <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">Nice model</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thommes1999_141-3"><a href="#cite_note-Thommes1999-141">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Moons">Moons</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Uranus" title="Moons of Uranus">Moons of Uranus</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_discovery_of_Solar_System_planets_and_their_natural_satellites" title="Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their natural satellites">Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their natural satellites</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:319px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranian_moon_montage.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="2537" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Uranian_moon_montage.png/317px-Uranian_moon_montage.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Uranian_moon_montage.png/476px-Uranian_moon_montage.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Uranian_moon_montage.png/634px-Uranian_moon_montage.png 2x" width="317"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranian_moon_montage.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major moons of Uranus in order of increasing distance (left to right), at their proper relative sizes and <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedos</a> (collage of <i>Voyager 2</i> photographs)</div></div></div><p>Uranus has 27 known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_satellites" title="Natural satellites">natural satellites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard_Jewitt_Kleyna_2006_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard_Jewitt_Kleyna_2006-146">[137]</a></sup> The names of these satellites are chosen from characters in the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pope" title="Alexander Pope">Alexander Pope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faure2007_83-4"><a href="#cite_note-Faure2007-83">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nineplanets_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nineplanets-147">[138]</a></sup> The five main satellites are <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faure2007_83-5"><a href="#cite_note-Faure2007-83">[75]</a></sup> The Uranian satellite system is the least massive among those of the giant planets; the combined mass of the five major satellites would be less than half that of <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> (largest moon of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>) alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_Campbell_et_al._1992_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson_Campbell_et_al._1992-15">[12]</a></sup> The largest of Uranus's satellites, Titania, has a radius of only 788.9 km (490.2 mi), or less than half that of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, but slightly more than Rhea, the second-largest satellite of Saturn, making Titania the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites_by_diameter" title="List of natural satellites by diameter">eighth-largest moon</a> in the Solar System. Uranus's satellites have relatively low <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedos</a>; ranging from 0.20 for Umbriel to 0.35 for Ariel (in green light).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup> They are ice–rock conglomerates composed of roughly 50% ice and 50% rock. The ice may include ammonia and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-summary_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-summary-124">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann2006_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hussmann2006-148">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the Uranian satellites, Ariel appears to have the youngest surface, with the fewest impact craters, and Umbriel the oldest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-7"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-summary_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-summary-124">[115]</a></sup> Miranda has fault canyons 20 km (12 mi) deep, terraced layers, and a chaotic variation in surface ages and features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-8"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup> Miranda's past geologic activity is thought to have been driven by <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">tidal heating</a> at a time when its orbit was more eccentric than currently, probably as a result of a former 3:1 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> with Umbriel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tittemore_Wisdom_1990_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tittemore_Wisdom_1990-149">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rift" title="Rift">Extensional</a> processes associated with upwelling <a href="/wiki/Diapir" title="Diapir">diapirs</a> are the likely origin of Miranda's 'racetrack'-like <a href="/wiki/Corona_(planetary_geology)" title="Corona (planetary geology)">coronae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[142]</a></sup> Ariel is thought to have once been held in a 4:1 resonance with Titania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tittemore_1990_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tittemore_1990-152">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranus has at least one <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_orbit" title="Horseshoe orbit">horseshoe orbiter</a> occupying the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>–Uranus <a href="/wiki/Lagrange_point#L3" title="Lagrange point">L<sub>3</sub></a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian point</a>—a gravitationally unstable region at 180° in its orbit, <a href="/wiki/83982_Crantor" title="83982 Crantor">83982 Crantor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coorbital1_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-coorbital1-153">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coorbital2_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-coorbital2-154">[145]</a></sup> Crantor moves inside Uranus's co-orbital region on a complex, temporary horseshoe orbit.
<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2010_EU65" title="2010 EU65">2010 EU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">65</span></span></a></span> is also a promising Uranus horseshoe <a href="/wiki/Trojan_(celestial_body)" title="Trojan (celestial body)">librator</a> candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coorbital2_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-coorbital2-154">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rings">Rings</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Uranus" title="Rings of Uranus">Rings of Uranus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranuslight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="602" data-file-width="602" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Uranuslight.jpg/220px-Uranuslight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Uranuslight.jpg/330px-Uranuslight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Uranuslight.jpg/440px-Uranuslight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranuslight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uranus's aurorae against its equatorial rings, imaged by the Hubble telescope. Unlike the aurorae of Earth and Jupiter, those of Uranus are not in line with its poles, due to its lopsided magnetic field.</div></div></div>
<p>The Uranian rings are composed of extremely dark particles, which vary in size from micrometres to a fraction of a metre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-9"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup> Thirteen distinct rings are presently known, the brightest being the ε ring. All except two rings of Uranus are extremely narrow – they are usually a few kilometres wide. The rings are probably quite young; the dynamics considerations indicate that they did not form with Uranus. The matter in the rings may once have been part of a moon (or moons) that was shattered by high-speed impacts. From numerous pieces of debris that formed as a result of those impacts, only a few particles survived, in stable zones corresponding to the locations of the present rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-summary_124-3"><a href="#cite_note-summary-124">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito2002_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito2002-155">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>William Herschel described a possible ring around Uranus in 1789. This sighting is generally considered doubtful, because the rings are quite faint, and in the two following centuries none were noted by other observers. Still, Herschel made an accurate description of the epsilon ring's size, its angle relative to Earth, its red colour, and its apparent changes as Uranus travelled around the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[148]</a></sup> The ring system was definitively discovered on 10 March 1977 by <a href="/wiki/James_L._Elliot" title="James L. Elliot">James L. Elliot</a>, Edward W. Dunham, and <a href="/wiki/Jessica_Mink" title="Jessica Mink">Jessica Mink</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_Airborne_Observatory" title="Kuiper Airborne Observatory">Kuiper Airborne Observatory</a>. The discovery was serendipitous; they planned to use the <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a> of the star SAO 158687 (also known as HD 128598) by Uranus to study its <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. When their observations were analysed, they found that the star had disappeared briefly from view five times both before and after it disappeared behind Uranus. They concluded that there must be a ring system around Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elliot1977_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elliot1977-158">[149]</a></sup> Later they detected four additional rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elliot1977_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elliot1977-158">[149]</a></sup> The rings were directly imaged when <i>Voyager 2</i> passed Uranus in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-10"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup> <i>Voyager 2</i> also discovered two additional faint rings, bringing the total number to eleven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-11"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> detected a pair of previously unknown rings. The largest is located twice as far from Uranus as the previously known rings. These new rings are so far from Uranus that they are called the "outer" ring system. Hubble also spotted two small satellites, one of which, <a href="/wiki/Mab_(moon)" title="Mab (moon)">Mab</a>, shares its orbit with the outermost newly discovered ring. The new rings bring the total number of Uranian rings to 13.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[150]</a></sup> In April 2006, images of the new rings from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keck_Observatory" title="Keck Observatory">Keck Observatory</a> yielded the colours of the outer rings: the outermost is blue and the other one red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dePater2006_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-dePater2006-160">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[152]</a></sup> One hypothesis concerning the outer ring's blue colour is that it is composed of minute particles of water ice from the surface of Mab that are small enough to scatter blue light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dePater2006_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-dePater2006-160">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[153]</a></sup> In contrast, Uranus's inner rings appear grey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dePater2006_160-2"><a href="#cite_note-dePater2006-160">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Uranus" title="Exploration of Uranus">Exploration of Uranus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_Final_Image.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="794" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Uranus_Final_Image.jpg/170px-Uranus_Final_Image.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Uranus_Final_Image.jpg/255px-Uranus_Final_Image.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Uranus_Final_Image.jpg/340px-Uranus_Final_Image.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_Final_Image.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crescent Uranus as imaged by <i>Voyager 2</i> while en route to Neptune</div></div></div>
<p>In 1986, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> interplanetary probe encountered Uranus. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">flyby</a> remains the only investigation of Uranus carried out from a short distance and no other visits are planned. <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> was unable to visit Uranus because investigation of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>'s moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> was considered a priority. This trajectory took <i>Voyager 1</i> out of the plane of the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>, ending its planetary science mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swift1997_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swift1997-163">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 118">: 118 </span></sup> Launched in 1977, <i>Voyager 2</i> made its closest approach to Uranus on 24 January 1986, coming within 81,500 km (50,600 mi) of the cloudtops, before continuing its journey to Neptune. The spacecraft studied the structure and chemical composition of Uranus's atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler_1986_102-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler_1986-102">[94]</a></sup> including its unique weather, caused by its axial tilt of 97.77°. It made the first detailed investigations of its five largest moons and discovered 10 new ones. Voyager 2 examined all nine of the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Uranus" title="Rings of Uranus">system's known rings</a> and discovered two more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_26-12"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-26">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-summary_124-4"><a href="#cite_note-summary-124">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[155]</a></sup> It also studied the magnetic field, its irregular structure, its tilt and its unique corkscrew <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetotail</a> caused by Uranus's sideways orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_117-9"><a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-117">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The possibility of sending the <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens"><i>Cassini</i> spacecraft</a> from Saturn to Uranus was evaluated during a mission extension planning phase in 2009, but was ultimately rejected in favour of destroying it in the Saturnian atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spilker_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-spilker-165">[156]</a></sup> It would have taken about twenty years to get to the Uranian system after departing Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spilker_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-spilker-165">[156]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranus_orbiter_and_probe" title="Uranus orbiter and probe">Uranus orbiter and probe</a> was recommended by the 2013–2022 <a href="/wiki/Planetary_Science_Decadal_Survey" title="Planetary Science Decadal Survey">Planetary Science Decadal Survey</a> published in 2011; the proposal envisages launch during 2020–2023 and a 13-year cruise to Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uop_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-uop-166">[157]</a></sup> A Uranus entry probe could use <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Venus_Multiprobe" title="Pioneer Venus Multiprobe">Pioneer Venus Multiprobe</a> heritage and descend to 1–5 atmospheres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uop_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-uop-166">[157]</a></sup> The ESA evaluated a "medium-class" mission called <a href="/wiki/Uranus_Pathfinder" title="Uranus Pathfinder">Uranus Pathfinder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[158]</a></sup> A New Frontiers Uranus Orbiter has been evaluated and recommended in the study, <i>The Case for a Uranus Orbiter</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hof_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-hof-168">[159]</a></sup> Such a mission is aided by the ease with which a relatively big mass can be sent to the system—over 1500 kg with an <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas 521</a> and 12-year journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hof2_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-hof2-169">[160]</a></sup> For more concepts see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proposed_Uranus_missions" title="Proposed Uranus missions">proposed Uranus missions</a>.
</p><p>In April, 2022, the next <a href="/wiki/Planetary_Science_Decadal_Survey" title="Planetary Science Decadal Survey">Planetary Science Decadal Survey</a> placed its highest priority for the next "flagship" project on a full package mission (orbiter and probe) to Uranus, with a projected launch window starting in 2031. The "dearth" of <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giant</a> science was key to its prioritization. Another key issue was that such a mission would use extant technology, and not require development of other instruments and systems to be successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h2>
<ul><li>In <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrology</a>, the planet Uranus (symbol <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_monogram.svg"><img alt="Uranus's astrological symbol" data-file-height="15" data-file-width="15" decoding="async" height="22" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Uranus_monogram.svg/22px-Uranus_monogram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Uranus_monogram.svg/33px-Uranus_monogram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Uranus_monogram.svg/44px-Uranus_monogram.svg.png 2x" width="22"/></a>) is the ruling planet of <a href="/wiki/Aquarius_(astrology)" title="Aquarius (astrology)">Aquarius</a>. Because Uranus is <a href="/wiki/Cyan" title="Cyan">cyan</a> and Uranus is associated with electricity, the colour <a href="/wiki/Electric_blue_(color)" title="Electric blue (color)">electric blue</a>, which is close to cyan, is associated with the sign Aquarius<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[162]</a></sup> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranus_(astrology)" title="Uranus (astrology)">Uranus in astrology</a>).</li>
<li>The chemical element <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, discovered in 1789 by the German chemist <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth" title="Martin Heinrich Klaproth">Martin Heinrich Klaproth</a>, was named after the then-newly discovered Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[163]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lydia_Sigourney" title="Lydia Sigourney">Lydia Sigourney</a> included her poem <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikisource-logo.svg"><img alt="Wikisource-logo.svg" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" decoding="async" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/12px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/18px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/24px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/></a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Poems_Sigourney_1827/The_Georgian_Planet" title="s:Poems Sigourney 1827/The Georgian Planet">The Georgian Planet</a>. in her 1827 collection of poetry.</li>
<li>"Uranus, the Magician" is a movement in <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Holst" title="Gustav Holst">Gustav Holst</a>'s orchestral suite <i><a href="/wiki/The_Planets" title="The Planets">The Planets</a></i>, written between 1914 and 1916.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Operation_Uranus" title="Operation Uranus">Operation Uranus</a> was the successful <a href="/wiki/Military_operation" title="Military operation">military operation</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Red Army</a> to take back <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stalingrad" title="Stalingrad">Stalingrad</a> and marked the turning point in the land war against the <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a>.</li>
<li>The lines "Then felt I like some watcher of the skies/When a new planet swims into his ken", from <a href="/wiki/John_Keats" title="John Keats">John Keats</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/On_First_Looking_into_Chapman%27s_Homer" title="On First Looking into Chapman's Homer">On First Looking into Chapman's Homer</a>", are a reference to Herschel's discovery of Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[164]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> popular culture, humor is often derived from the common pronunciation of Uranus's name, which resembles that of the phrase "your <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[165]</a></sup></li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> These are the mean elements from VSOP87, together with derived quantities.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-atmospheric_pressure-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-atmospheric_pressure_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-atmospheric_pressure_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-atmospheric_pressure_13-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-atmospheric_pressure_13-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-atmospheric_pressure_13-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-atmospheric_pressure_13-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-atmospheric_pressure_13-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"> Refers to the level of 1 bar atmospheric pressure.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Calculated using data from Seidelmann, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seidelmann_Archinal_A'hearn_et_al._2007_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Seidelmann_Archinal_A'hearn_et_al._2007-12">[11]</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-atmospheric_pressure2-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-atmospheric_pressure2_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Based on the volume within the level of 1 bar atmospheric pressure.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Calculation of He, H<sub>2</sub> and CH<sub>4</sub> molar fractions is based on a 2.3% mixing ratio of methane to hydrogen and the 15/85 He/H<sub>2</sub> proportions measured at the tropopause. </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Because, in the English-speaking world, the latter sounds like "your <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a>", the former pronunciation also saves embarrassment: as <a href="/wiki/Pamela_L._Gay" title="Pamela L. Gay">Pamela Gay</a>, an astronomer at <a href="/wiki/Southern_Illinois_University_Edwardsville" title="Southern Illinois University Edwardsville">Southern Illinois University Edwardsville</a>, noted on her podcast, to avoid "being made fun of by any small schoolchildren ... when in doubt, don't emphasise anything and just say <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ˈjʊərənəs/</span>. And then run, quickly."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[41]</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-symbol_later-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-symbol_later_55-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Cf. <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_symbol_(fixed_width).svg" title="Platinum symbol for Uranus"><img alt="⛢" data-file-height="15" data-file-width="15" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/20px-Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/30px-Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/40px-Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a> (not supported by all fonts)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-symbol_first-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-symbol_first_59-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Cf. <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_monogram_(fixed_width).svg" title="H monogram for Uranus"><img alt="♅" data-file-height="15" data-file-width="15" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg/20px-Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg/30px-Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg/40px-Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a> (not supported by all fonts)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Mixing ratio is defined as the number of molecules of a compound per a molecule of hydrogen. </span>
</li>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">

Because the vowel <i>a</i> is short in both Greek and Latin, the former pronunciation, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ˈjʊərənəs/</span>, is the expected one. Bretagnon's complete VSOP87 model. It gives the 17th @ 18.283075301au. <a class="external free" href="http://vo.imcce.fr/webservices/miriade/?forms" rel="nofollow">http://vo.imcce.fr/webservices/miriade/?forms</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210907050257/http://vo.imcce.fr/webservices/miriade/?forms" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 7 September 2021 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> IMCCE  Observatoire de Paris / CNRS  Calculated for a series of dates, five or ten days apart, in August 2050, using an interpolation formula from <i>Astronomical Algorithms</i>. Perihelion came very early on the 17th. INPOP planetary theory</span>

<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060210222142/http://vesuvius.jsc.nasa.gov/er/seh/hersc.html" rel="nofollow">"Voyager at Uranus"</a>. <i>NASA JPL</i>. <b>7</b> (85): 400–268. 1986. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://vesuvius.jsc.nasa.gov/er/seh/hersc.html" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 10 February 2006.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=NASA+JPL&amp;rft.atitle=Voyager+at+Uranus&amp;rft.volume=7&amp;rft.issue=85&amp;rft.pages=400-268&amp;rft.date=1986&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fvesuvius.jsc.nasa.gov%2Fer%2Fseh%2Fhersc.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AUranus"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Francisca-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Francisca_50-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Francisca_50-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFHerschel,_Francisca1917">Herschel, Francisca (1917). "The meaning of the symbol H+o for the planet Uranus". <i>The Observatory</i>. <b>40</b>: 306. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier)" title="Bibcode (identifier)">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1917Obs....40..306H" rel="nofollow">1917Obs....40..306H</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Observatory&amp;rft.atitle=The+meaning+of+the+symbol+H%2Bo+for+the+planet+Uranus&amp;rft.volume=40&amp;rft.pages=306&amp;rft.date=1917&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1917Obs....40..306H&amp;rft.au=Herschel%2C+Francisca&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AUranus"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Bode-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Bode_51-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bode_51-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bode_51-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBode1784">Bode 1784</a>, pp. 88–90: [In original German]: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Bereits in der am 12ten März 1782 bei der hiesigen naturforschenden Gesellschaft vorgelesenen Abhandlung, habe ich den Namen des Vaters vom Saturn, nemlich Uranos, oder wie er mit der lateinischen Endung gewöhnlicher ist, Uranus vorgeschlagen, und habe seit dem das Vergnügen gehabt, daß verschiedene Astronomen und Mathematiker in ihren Schriften oder in Briefen an mich, diese Benennung aufgenommen oder gebilligt. Meines Erachtens muß man bei dieser Wahl die Mythologie befolgen, aus welcher die uralten Namen der übrigen Planeten entlehnen worden; denn in der Reihe der bisher bekannten, würde der von einer merkwürdigen Person oder Begebenheit der neuern Zeit wahrgenommene Name eines Planeten sehr auffallen. Diodor von Cicilien erzahlt die Geschichte der Atlanten, eines uralten Volks, welches eine der fruchtbarsten Gegenden in Africa bewohnte, und die Meeresküsten seines Landes als das Vaterland der Götter ansah. Uranus war ihr, erster König, Stifter ihres gesitteter Lebens und Erfinder vieler nützlichen Künste. Zugleich wird er auch als ein fleißiger und geschickter Himmelsforscher des Alterthums beschrieben... Noch mehr: Uranus war der Vater des Saturns und des Atlas, so wie der erstere der Vater des Jupiters.</i></span></p></blockquote> [Translated]: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Already in the pre-read at the local Natural History Society on 12th March 1782 treatise, I have the father's name from Saturn, namely Uranos, or as it is usually with the Latin suffix, proposed Uranus, and have since had the pleasure that various astronomers and mathematicians, cited in their writings or letters to me approving this designation. In my view, it is necessary to follow the mythology in this election, which had been borrowed from the ancient name of the other planets; because in the series of previously known, perceived by a strange person or event of modern times name of a planet would very noticeable. Diodorus of Cilicia tells the story of Atlas, an ancient people that inhabited one of the most fertile areas in Africa, and looked at the sea shores of his country as the homeland of the gods. Uranus was her first king, founder of their civilized life and inventor of many useful arts. At the same time he is also described as a diligent and skilful astronomers of antiquity ... even more: Uranus was the father of Saturn and the Atlas, as the former is the father of Jupiter.</p></blockquote></span>
</li>
